China finds Trump difficult to tackle owing to his unpredictability and refusal to play by the established rules, rules that China has spent decades in manipulating. China also finds it discomfiting that for the leader of a democracy, Trump has a higher threshold of pain and is driven by a sense of manifest destiny. This allows Trump, who has suffered two assassination attempts, to take steps that are seemingly impossible or at least difficult for a leader who functions within the parameters of an electoral democracy. Trump‚Äôs uncertainty, unpredictability, greater latitude, and the ability to absorb criticisms pose a particularly tricky challenge for Xi. If the Chinese president retaliates and matches Trump in countermeasures, he risks exposing China‚Äôs rickety economy to more external shocks. If Xi appears to back down, that could send a message of ‚Äòweakness‚Äô, a fatal quality in a CCP general secretary. That doesn‚Äôt mean Xi is sitting idle. It has been fascinating to watch the Chinese retaliation, calibrated, targeted and relentless. Cognizant of America‚Äôs endeavour to isolate China, Xi has gone on a diplomatic charm offensive. The ‚Äòwolf warrior‚Äô has transformed into an ‚Äòaffable‚Äô Xi, trying to win friends and influence people. The Chinese president is wooing the Europeans, trying to wean the bloc away from America‚Äôs influence. Xi recently hosted the Spanish president, Pedro Sanchez, and urged the EU to join forces with China to ‚Äòdefend globalization‚Äô and resist ‚Äúunilateral acts of bullying‚Äù, a thin reference to Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Europeans, anxious with the antics of a mercurial American president whom they do not trust, are in turn gravitating closer to their ‚Äúsystemic rival‚Äù, easing regulations on Chinese-made electric vehicles and planning a trip to Beijing in July for a summit meeting with Xi. Self-interest is thicker than ‚Äòvalues‚Äô and ‚Äòprinciples‚Äô. As Xi‚Äôs commerce minister was working the phone lines to parley with European and ASEAN officials, the Chinese president went recently on a Southeast Asia tour, seeking to draw Cambodia, Malaysia and Vietnam closer to form a broader coalition against Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Xi‚Äôs aim was to portray China as the defender of the global trading system that these nations have benefitted from, and paint China as an image of stability and reliability amid global uncertainty. His task was also to ensure that these Southeast Asian nations do not give in to Trump‚Äôs demands of cutting China off from their economies or impose taxes of their own on Chinese goods. China has also sought to present a friendlier face to India. If you tell me that Beijing‚Äôs moves to placate New Delhi over a $100 billion trade surplus and promise of buying more ‚Äúpremium products‚Äù from Indian manufacturers (that it is yet to act on) has nothing to Trump‚Äôs belligerence, then I have a bridge to sell you. Alongside, China has raised duties on American products to 125%, carefully targeting the Red States ‚Äì blocking natural gas, Boeing jets, beef, poultry and bone meals along with export restrictions on rare earths. These moves are designed to hit specific industries rooted in the MAGA base and exploit America‚Äôs dependence on critical minerals needed for modern appliances, medical and defence technology. These elaborate and intricate retaliatory mechanisms adopted by China suggest that Xi is in a precarious spot, waiting for his opponent to blink in a costly game of poker. Not only does he face an economic crisis that cannot be wished away, he is also struggling with another eventuality ‚Äì the inevitable fallout of adopting deception as the fulcrum of state policy is that the Chinese Communist Party has made China friendless, and though it runs trade surplus with many nations, it runs trust deficit with everyone. Australia, Japan and South Korea have already rejected his calls of forming a broad coalition against Trump administration, the Southeast Asian nations and the European Union are hedging their bets. On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. At the recent Canton trade fair, China‚Äôs biggest expo held twice a year in the city of Guangzhou where Chinese manufacturers, all 30,000 of them showcase their products to clients around the globe ‚Äì a healthy chunk from America ‚Äì the signs of distress were overwhelming. Financial Times spotted a notice by the organisers of the fair, started by Mao Zedong in 1957, describing the current trade scenario as ‚Äúgrim and complex‚Äù and warning exhibitors that they would carry out inspections at the end of each of the fair‚Äôs three stages to ensure that ‚Äúnone packed up early‚Äù. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down. üá®üá≥ pic.twitter.com/vPgifasYmI ‚Äî Mao Ning ÊØõÂÆÅ (@SpoxCHN_MaoNing) April 10, 2025 The shrillness can barely conceal the nervousness beneath. Trump‚Äôs uncertainty, unpredictability, greater latitude, and the ability to absorb criticisms pose a particularly tricky challenge for Xi. If Xi appears to back down, that could send a message of ‚Äòweakness‚Äô, a fatal quality in a CCP general secretary. That doesn‚Äôt mean Xi is sitting idle. It has been fascinating to watch the Chinese retaliation, calibrated, targeted and relentless. Cognizant of America‚Äôs endeavour to isolate China, Xi has gone on a diplomatic charm offensive. The ‚Äòwolf warrior‚Äô has transformed into an ‚Äòaffable‚Äô Xi, trying to win friends and influence people. The Chinese president is wooing the Europeans, trying to wean the bloc away from America‚Äôs influence. Xi recently hosted the Spanish president, Pedro Sanchez, and urged the EU to join forces with China to ‚Äòdefend globalization‚Äô and resist ‚Äúunilateral acts of bullying‚Äù, a thin reference to Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Europeans, anxious with the antics of a mercurial American president whom they do not trust, are in turn gravitating closer to their ‚Äúsystemic rival‚Äù, easing regulations on Chinese-made electric vehicles and planning a trip to Beijing in July for a summit meeting with Xi. Self-interest is thicker than ‚Äòvalues‚Äô and ‚Äòprinciples‚Äô. As Xi‚Äôs commerce minister was working the phone lines to parley with European and ASEAN officials, the Chinese president went recently on a Southeast Asia tour, seeking to draw Cambodia, Malaysia and Vietnam closer to form a broader coalition against Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Xi‚Äôs aim was to portray China as the defender of the global trading system that these nations have benefitted from, and paint China as an image of stability and reliability amid global uncertainty. His task was also to ensure that these Southeast Asian nations do not give in to Trump‚Äôs demands of cutting China off from their economies or impose taxes of their own on Chinese goods. China has also sought to present a friendlier face to India. If you tell me that Beijing‚Äôs moves to placate New Delhi over a $100 billion trade surplus and promise of buying more ‚Äúpremium products‚Äù from Indian manufacturers (that it is yet to act on) has nothing to Trump‚Äôs belligerence, then I have a bridge to sell you. Alongside, China has raised duties on American products to 125%, carefully targeting the Red States ‚Äì blocking natural gas, Boeing jets, beef, poultry and bone meals along with export restrictions on rare earths. These moves are designed to hit specific industries rooted in the MAGA base and exploit America‚Äôs dependence on critical minerals needed for modern appliances, medical and defence technology. These elaborate and intricate retaliatory mechanisms adopted by China suggest that Xi is in a precarious spot, waiting for his opponent to blink in a costly game of poker. Not only does he face an economic crisis that cannot be wished away, he is also struggling with another eventuality ‚Äì the inevitable fallout of adopting deception as the fulcrum of state policy is that the Chinese Communist Party has made China friendless, and though it runs trade surplus with many nations, it runs trust deficit with everyone. Australia, Japan and South Korea have already rejected his calls of forming a broad coalition against Trump administration, the Southeast Asian nations and the European Union are hedging their bets. On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. At the recent Canton trade fair, China‚Äôs biggest expo held twice a year in the city of Guangzhou where Chinese manufacturers, all 30,000 of them showcase their products to clients around the globe ‚Äì a healthy chunk from America ‚Äì the signs of distress were overwhelming. Financial Times spotted a notice by the organisers of the fair, started by Mao Zedong in 1957, describing the current trade scenario as ‚Äúgrim and complex‚Äù and warning exhibitors that they would carry out inspections at the end of each of the fair‚Äôs three stages to ensure that ‚Äúnone packed up early‚Äù. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down. üá®üá≥ pic.twitter.com/vPgifasYmI ‚Äî Mao Ning ÊØõÂÆÅ (@SpoxCHN_MaoNing) April 10, 2025 The shrillness can barely conceal the nervousness beneath. Just like Mexico, that has reportedly offered to match American levies on China to strike a deal with Trump, Vietnam, faced with the prospect of 46% US tariffs, sent a team to Washington and promised to crack down on ‚Äútrade fraud‚Äù, a reference to millions of Chinese goods routed to the US through its territory to avoid American taxes. Chinese tactics range from establishing factories in Vietnam, using Vietnamese workers to process goods for the ‚Äòmade in Vietnam‚Äô label, or as Reuters reports, making ships carrying Chinese-made goods dwell in Vietnamese ports ‚Äújust long enough to obtain documents certifying that the products were made in Vietnam before leaving.‚Äù Little wonder that Vietnam now has the fourth-highest trade surplus with the US, next only to China, Mexico and the EU. If these routes are blocked, China would obviously be in deeper trouble. China manufactures far more products than its consumers can absorb, making exports imperative for the wheels of its economy to run. The deflation loop that it suffers from owing to its crisis-ridden real estate sector, where most of the middle-class wealth is parked, is exacerbated by the fact that its export volume far outstrips prices, adding to deflationary pressure. This is exactly where Trump is stress-testing the Chinese economy by levying eye-watering taxes on exports, attempting to shift the entire global trade architecture from ‚Äòopen‚Äô to one based on bilateral ‚Äòdeals‚Äô and collective coercive mechanisms. China finds Trump difficult to tackle owing to his unpredictability and refusal to play by the established rules, rules that China has spent decades in manipulating. These elaborate and intricate retaliatory mechanisms adopted by China suggest that Xi is in a precarious spot, waiting for his opponent to blink in a costly game of poker. Not only does he face an economic crisis that cannot be wished away, he is also struggling with another eventuality ‚Äì the inevitable fallout of adopting deception as the fulcrum of state policy is that the Chinese Communist Party has made China friendless, and though it runs trade surplus with many nations, it runs trust deficit with everyone. Australia, Japan and South Korea have already rejected his calls of forming a broad coalition against Trump administration, the Southeast Asian nations and the European Union are hedging their bets. On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. At the recent Canton trade fair, China‚Äôs biggest expo held twice a year in the city of Guangzhou where Chinese manufacturers, all 30,000 of them showcase their products to clients around the globe ‚Äì a healthy chunk from America ‚Äì the signs of distress were overwhelming. Financial Times spotted a notice by the organisers of the fair, started by Mao Zedong in 1957, describing the current trade scenario as ‚Äúgrim and complex‚Äù and warning exhibitors that they would carry out inspections at the end of each of the fair‚Äôs three stages to ensure that ‚Äúnone packed up early‚Äù. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down. üá®üá≥ pic.twitter.com/vPgifasYmI ‚Äî Mao Ning ÊØõÂÆÅ (@SpoxCHN_MaoNing) April 10, 2025 The shrillness can barely conceal the nervousness beneath. Free Republic Browse ¬∑ Search News/Activism Topics ¬∑ Post Article FreeRepublic, LLC, PO BOX 9771, FRESNO, CA 93794 FreeRepublic.com is powered by software copyright 2000-2008 John Robinson This is exactly where Trump is stress-testing the Chinese economy by levying eye-watering taxes on exports, attempting to shift the entire global trade architecture from ‚Äòopen‚Äô to one based on bilateral ‚Äòdeals‚Äô and collective coercive mechanisms. China finds Trump difficult to tackle owing to his unpredictability and refusal to play by the established rules, rules that China has spent decades in manipulating. China also finds it discomfiting that for the leader of a democracy, Trump has a higher threshold of pain and is driven by a sense of manifest destiny. This allows Trump, who has suffered two assassination attempts, to take steps that are seemingly impossible or at least difficult for a leader who functions within the parameters of an electoral democracy. Trump‚Äôs uncertainty, unpredictability, greater latitude, and the ability to absorb criticisms pose a particularly tricky challenge for Xi. If the Chinese president retaliates and matches Trump in countermeasures, he risks exposing China‚Äôs rickety economy to more external shocks. If Xi appears to back down, that could send a message of ‚Äòweakness‚Äô, a fatal quality in a CCP general secretary. That doesn‚Äôt mean Xi is sitting idle. It has been fascinating to watch the Chinese retaliation, calibrated, targeted and relentless. Cognizant of America‚Äôs endeavour to isolate China, Xi has gone on a diplomatic charm offensive. The ‚Äòwolf warrior‚Äô has transformed into an ‚Äòaffable‚Äô Xi, trying to win friends and influence people. The Chinese president is wooing the Europeans, trying to wean the bloc away from America‚Äôs influence. Xi recently hosted the Spanish president, Pedro Sanchez, and urged the EU to join forces with China to ‚Äòdefend globalization‚Äô and resist ‚Äúunilateral acts of bullying‚Äù, a thin reference to Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Europeans, anxious with the antics of a mercurial American president whom they do not trust, are in turn gravitating closer to their ‚Äúsystemic rival‚Äù, easing regulations on Chinese-made electric vehicles and planning a trip to Beijing in July for a summit meeting with Xi. Self-interest is thicker than ‚Äòvalues‚Äô and ‚Äòprinciples‚Äô. As Xi‚Äôs commerce minister was working the phone lines to parley with European and ASEAN officials, the Chinese president went recently on a Southeast Asia tour, seeking to draw Cambodia, Malaysia and Vietnam closer to form a broader coalition against Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Xi‚Äôs aim was to portray China as the defender of the global trading system that these nations have benefitted from, and paint China as an image of stability and reliability amid global uncertainty. His task was also to ensure that these Southeast Asian nations do not give in to Trump‚Äôs demands of cutting China off from their economies or impose taxes of their own on Chinese goods. China has also sought to present a friendlier face to India. If you tell me that Beijing‚Äôs moves to placate New Delhi over a $100 billion trade surplus and promise of buying more ‚Äúpremium products‚Äù from Indian manufacturers (that it is yet to act on) has nothing to Trump‚Äôs belligerence, then I have a bridge to sell you. Alongside, China has raised duties on American products to 125%, carefully targeting the Red States ‚Äì blocking natural gas, Boeing jets, beef, poultry and bone meals along with export restrictions on rare earths. These moves are designed to hit specific industries rooted in the MAGA base and exploit America‚Äôs dependence on critical minerals needed for modern appliances, medical and defence technology. These elaborate and intricate retaliatory mechanisms adopted by China suggest that Xi is in a precarious spot, waiting for his opponent to blink in a costly game of poker. Not only does he face an economic crisis that cannot be wished away, he is also struggling with another eventuality ‚Äì the inevitable fallout of adopting deception as the fulcrum of state policy is that the Chinese Communist Party has made China friendless, and though it runs trade surplus with many nations, it runs trust deficit with everyone. Australia, Japan and South Korea have already rejected his calls of forming a broad coalition against Trump administration, the Southeast Asian nations and the European Union are hedging their bets. On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. Free Republic Browse ¬∑ Search News/Activism Topics ¬∑ Post Article FreeRepublic, LLC, PO BOX 9771, FRESNO, CA 93794 FreeRepublic.com is powered by software copyright 2000-2008 John Robinson That doesn‚Äôt mean Xi is sitting idle. It has been fascinating to watch the Chinese retaliation, calibrated, targeted and relentless. Cognizant of America‚Äôs endeavour to isolate China, Xi has gone on a diplomatic charm offensive. The ‚Äòwolf warrior‚Äô has transformed into an ‚Äòaffable‚Äô Xi, trying to win friends and influence people. The Chinese president is wooing the Europeans, trying to wean the bloc away from America‚Äôs influence. Xi recently hosted the Spanish president, Pedro Sanchez, and urged the EU to join forces with China to ‚Äòdefend globalization‚Äô and resist ‚Äúunilateral acts of bullying‚Äù, a thin reference to Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Europeans, anxious with the antics of a mercurial American president whom they do not trust, are in turn gravitating closer to their ‚Äúsystemic rival‚Äù, easing regulations on Chinese-made electric vehicles and planning a trip to Beijing in July for a summit meeting with Xi. Self-interest is thicker than ‚Äòvalues‚Äô and ‚Äòprinciples‚Äô. As Xi‚Äôs commerce minister was working the phone lines to parley with European and ASEAN officials, the Chinese president went recently on a Southeast Asia tour, seeking to draw Cambodia, Malaysia and Vietnam closer to form a broader coalition against Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Xi‚Äôs aim was to portray China as the defender of the global trading system that these nations have benefitted from, and paint China as an image of stability and reliability amid global uncertainty. His task was also to ensure that these Southeast Asian nations do not give in to Trump‚Äôs demands of cutting China off from their economies or impose taxes of their own on Chinese goods. China has also sought to present a friendlier face to India. If you tell me that Beijing‚Äôs moves to placate New Delhi over a $100 billion trade surplus and promise of buying more ‚Äúpremium products‚Äù from Indian manufacturers (that it is yet to act on) has nothing to Trump‚Äôs belligerence, then I have a bridge to sell you. Alongside, China has raised duties on American products to 125%, carefully targeting the Red States ‚Äì blocking natural gas, Boeing jets, beef, poultry and bone meals along with export restrictions on rare earths. These moves are designed to hit specific industries rooted in the MAGA base and exploit America‚Äôs dependence on critical minerals needed for modern appliances, medical and defence technology. These elaborate and intricate retaliatory mechanisms adopted by China suggest that Xi is in a precarious spot, waiting for his opponent to blink in a costly game of poker. Not only does he face an economic crisis that cannot be wished away, he is also struggling with another eventuality ‚Äì the inevitable fallout of adopting deception as the fulcrum of state policy is that the Chinese Communist Party has made China friendless, and though it runs trade surplus with many nations, it runs trust deficit with everyone. Australia, Japan and South Korea have already rejected his calls of forming a broad coalition against Trump administration, the Southeast Asian nations and the European Union are hedging their bets. On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. At the recent Canton trade fair, China‚Äôs biggest expo held twice a year in the city of Guangzhou where Chinese manufacturers, all 30,000 of them showcase their products to clients around the globe ‚Äì a healthy chunk from America ‚Äì the signs of distress were overwhelming. Financial Times spotted a notice by the organisers of the fair, started by Mao Zedong in 1957, describing the current trade scenario as ‚Äúgrim and complex‚Äù and warning exhibitors that they would carry out inspections at the end of each of the fair‚Äôs three stages to ensure that ‚Äúnone packed up early‚Äù. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down. üá®üá≥ pic.twitter.com/vPgifasYmI ‚Äî Mao Ning ÊØõÂÆÅ (@SpoxCHN_MaoNing) April 10, 2025 The shrillness can barely conceal the nervousness beneath. Free Republic Browse ¬∑ Search News/Activism Topics ¬∑ Post Article FreeRepublic, LLC, PO BOX 9771, FRESNO, CA 93794 FreeRepublic.com is powered by software copyright 2000-2008 John Robinson Europeans, anxious with the antics of a mercurial American president whom they do not trust, are in turn gravitating closer to their ‚Äúsystemic rival‚Äù, easing regulations on Chinese-made electric vehicles and planning a trip to Beijing in July for a summit meeting with Xi. Self-interest is thicker than ‚Äòvalues‚Äô and ‚Äòprinciples‚Äô. As Xi‚Äôs commerce minister was working the phone lines to parley with European and ASEAN officials, the Chinese president went recently on a Southeast Asia tour, seeking to draw Cambodia, Malaysia and Vietnam closer to form a broader coalition against Trump‚Äôs tariffs. Xi‚Äôs aim was to portray China as the defender of the global trading system that these nations have benefitted from, and paint China as an image of stability and reliability amid global uncertainty. His task was also to ensure that these Southeast Asian nations do not give in to Trump‚Äôs demands of cutting China off from their economies or impose taxes of their own on Chinese goods. China has also sought to present a friendlier face to India. If you tell me that Beijing‚Äôs moves to placate New Delhi over a $100 billion trade surplus and promise of buying more ‚Äúpremium products‚Äù from Indian manufacturers (that it is yet to act on) has nothing to Trump‚Äôs belligerence, then I have a bridge to sell you. Alongside, China has raised duties on American products to 125%, carefully targeting the Red States ‚Äì blocking natural gas, Boeing jets, beef, poultry and bone meals along with export restrictions on rare earths. These moves are designed to hit specific industries rooted in the MAGA base and exploit America‚Äôs dependence on critical minerals needed for modern appliances, medical and defence technology. These elaborate and intricate retaliatory mechanisms adopted by China suggest that Xi is in a precarious spot, waiting for his opponent to blink in a costly game of poker. Not only does he face an economic crisis that cannot be wished away, he is also struggling with another eventuality ‚Äì the inevitable fallout of adopting deception as the fulcrum of state policy is that the Chinese Communist Party has made China friendless, and though it runs trade surplus with many nations, it runs trust deficit with everyone. Australia, Japan and South Korea have already rejected his calls of forming a broad coalition against Trump administration, the Southeast Asian nations and the European Union are hedging their bets. On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. At the recent Canton trade fair, China‚Äôs biggest expo held twice a year in the city of Guangzhou where Chinese manufacturers, all 30,000 of them showcase their products to clients around the globe ‚Äì a healthy chunk from America ‚Äì the signs of distress were overwhelming. Financial Times spotted a notice by the organisers of the fair, started by Mao Zedong in 1957, describing the current trade scenario as ‚Äúgrim and complex‚Äù and warning exhibitors that they would carry out inspections at the end of each of the fair‚Äôs three stages to ensure that ‚Äúnone packed up early‚Äù. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. Their model for maintaining power is to provide their people with just enough prosperity to keep them compliant, but not enough for them to be in a position to challenge the CCP‚Äôs authority. 5 posted on 04/21/2025 10:08:25 PM PDT by mbrfl [ Post Reply | Private Reply | To 1 | View Replies] To: Rockingham One hopes. 6 posted on 04/22/2025 1:14:15 AM PDT by sauropod (Make sure Satan has to climb over a lot of Scripture to get to you. Free Republic Browse ¬∑ Search News/Activism Topics ¬∑ Post Article FreeRepublic, LLC, PO BOX 9771, FRESNO, CA 93794 FreeRepublic.com is powered by software copyright 2000-2008 John Robinson Alongside, China has raised duties on American products to 125%, carefully targeting the Red States ‚Äì blocking natural gas, Boeing jets, beef, poultry and bone meals along with export restrictions on rare earths. These moves are designed to hit specific industries rooted in the MAGA base and exploit America‚Äôs dependence on critical minerals needed for modern appliances, medical and defence technology. These elaborate and intricate retaliatory mechanisms adopted by China suggest that Xi is in a precarious spot, waiting for his opponent to blink in a costly game of poker. Not only does he face an economic crisis that cannot be wished away, he is also struggling with another eventuality ‚Äì the inevitable fallout of adopting deception as the fulcrum of state policy is that the Chinese Communist Party has made China friendless, and though it runs trade surplus with many nations, it runs trust deficit with everyone. Australia, Japan and South Korea have already rejected his calls of forming a broad coalition against Trump administration, the Southeast Asian nations and the European Union are hedging their bets. On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. At the recent Canton trade fair, China‚Äôs biggest expo held twice a year in the city of Guangzhou where Chinese manufacturers, all 30,000 of them showcase their products to clients around the globe ‚Äì a healthy chunk from America ‚Äì the signs of distress were overwhelming. Financial Times spotted a notice by the organisers of the fair, started by Mao Zedong in 1957, describing the current trade scenario as ‚Äúgrim and complex‚Äù and warning exhibitors that they would carry out inspections at the end of each of the fair‚Äôs three stages to ensure that ‚Äúnone packed up early‚Äù. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down. üá®üá≥ pic.twitter.com/vPgifasYmI ‚Äî Mao Ning ÊØõÂÆÅ (@SpoxCHN_MaoNing) April 10, 2025 The shrillness can barely conceal the nervousness beneath. Their model for maintaining power is to provide their people with just enough prosperity to keep them compliant, but not enough for them to be in a position to challenge the CCP‚Äôs authority. 5 posted on 04/21/2025 10:08:25 PM PDT by mbrfl [ Post Reply | Private Reply | To 1 | View Replies] To: Rockingham One hopes. 6 posted on 04/22/2025 1:14:15 AM PDT by sauropod (Make sure Satan has to climb over a lot of Scripture to get to you. Free Republic Browse ¬∑ Search News/Activism Topics ¬∑ Post Article FreeRepublic, LLC, PO BOX 9771, FRESNO, CA 93794 FreeRepublic.com is powered by software copyright 2000-2008 John Robinson On the economic front, despite CCP‚Äôs tight control over information, it is difficult to hide the markers of trouble. At the recent Canton trade fair, China‚Äôs biggest expo held twice a year in the city of Guangzhou where Chinese manufacturers, all 30,000 of them showcase their products to clients around the globe ‚Äì a healthy chunk from America ‚Äì the signs of distress were overwhelming. Financial Times spotted a notice by the organisers of the fair, started by Mao Zedong in 1957, describing the current trade scenario as ‚Äúgrim and complex‚Äù and warning exhibitors that they would carry out inspections at the end of each of the fair‚Äôs three stages to ensure that ‚Äúnone packed up early‚Äù. The newspaper quoted many exporters at the fair, saying that that the new levies made selling to the US market unfeasible, with some commenting that ‚Äúall of our US customers have paused all of their orders‚Ä¶ the tariff is too high.‚Äù Reuters also spoke to a bunch of exhibitors. Most were too depressed over the proceedings, confirming that American orders ‚Äúhave either been delayed or stopped coming‚Äù, a matter of concern for the Chinese economy that‚Äôs more exposed to the vulnerabilities of an unstable global commerce than any other nation. Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down. üá®üá≥ pic.twitter.com/vPgifasYmI ‚Äî Mao Ning ÊØõÂÆÅ (@SpoxCHN_MaoNing) April 10, 2025 The shrillness can barely conceal the nervousness beneath. Free Republic Browse ¬∑ Search News/Activism Topics ¬∑ Post Article FreeRepublic, LLC, PO BOX 9771, FRESNO, CA 93794 FreeRepublic.com is powered by software copyright 2000-2008 John Robinson Some are looking to close their factories set up in Southeast Asian nations to bypass duties as Trump ratchets up tariffs across the board, while some are laying off employees, reducing management costs and cutting down on sundry expenses. Trump‚Äôs tariffs, according to another Reuters report, may ‚Äúslash Chinese exports to the US by 30%, cut overall exports by more than 4.5%, and drag economic growth by 1.3 percentage points.‚Äù It will be difficult for Chinese manufacturers, plagued with overcapacity, to cater to new markets of increased competition with the possibility looming large that many nations may raise their own barriers to stop dumping of redirected Chinese goods. One option that Xi in doubling on to escape the punch and clinch on Chinese economy, is spur domestic spending to absorb some of the overcapacity, given that exports to the US may tumble by around 80% over the next two years. The trouble with this plan is that Chinese consumers are unable to spend ‚Äì as a fall in consumer price index would indicate that declined for a second straight month according to latest data, and producer deflation that recorded a fall for the 29th straight month. Part of the reason Chinese domestic market cannot soak up excess capacity is that its middle-class wealth, tied up in the troubled real estate sector and stock markets is taking a severe beating in a society where ‚Äú70% of family assets are tied up in property. Every 5% decline in home prices will wipe out 19 trillion yuan ($2.7 trillion) in housing wealth, according to Bloomberg Economics.‚Äù While housing sector‚Äôs value may dramatically shrink in China‚Äôs GDP, the $2.9 trillion ‚Äòshadow bank‚Äô industry is suffering a collapse, deepening the wealth wipeout. Global banks are slashing China‚Äôs growth forecasts with UBS downgrading China‚Äôs 2025 growth forecast to 3.4% despite a 5.4% growth in first quarter, while the tariffs have squeezed low-end manufacturers‚Äô wafer-thin margin even tighter. Local governments, lacking in revenue streams, are too impoverished to provide support. FDI flow is at its lowest in three decades, while ‚Äúconfidence in the system among many in China‚Äôs elite has been shattered,‚Äù reported Bloomberg, quoting an investor who lost 16 million yuan ($2.2 million) from trust products. The report says he‚Äôs considering selling his family home in Beijing for a smaller apartment to raise cash. The CCP‚Äôs legitimacy is ultimately tied to China‚Äôs growth prospects, so the more economic challenges grow, unemployment prospects loom large, the hotter it may get for Xi under the collar with chances growing of internal dissent. Now, put in context the old clip that has resurfaced on American social media networks amplified by China‚Äôs foreign ministry, of Mao Zedong declaring that ‚ÄúWe are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. We don‚Äôt back down.‚Äù We are Chinese. We are not afraid of provocations. The Chinese policy of keeping its wealth under the control of the state rather than allowing it to be controlled or shared by the private sector is the basis for the CCP‚Äôs tight grip on power. Title: Tesla‚Äôs caught in a trade war trap. Blame Elon Musk
URL: https://www.cnn.com/2025/04/21/business/musk-tesla-investors-call/index.html
Time Published: 2025-04-21T09:43:49Z
Full Content:
Markets Hot Stocks Fear & Greed Index Latest Market News Hot Stocks Follow: Like many American manufacturers, Tesla is caught in a bind thanks to President Donald Trump‚Äôs trade war. Unlike most of those other companies, though, Tesla has an added wrinkle ‚Äì its CEO, Elon Musk, sometimes called the First Buddy for the close relationship he‚Äôs cultivated with the president. On Tuesday, Tesla reports earnings; that afternoon, Musk will take questions from investors. But there is a catch-22: If Musk continues his close ties to Trump, the company could alienate potential customers, both at home and overseas, who dislike Trump‚Äôs policies. If he distances himself from Trump, though, he risks the ire of the White House. It‚Äôs a lose-lose situation, and it‚Äôs a trap of Musk‚Äôs own making. The first quarter saw the company‚Äôs largest drop in sales after virtually nonstop double-digit sales growth. But the automaker‚Äôs finances are only part of what investors will want to know. Reassuring responses on any these topics could help reverse the slide that has wiped out about half of Tesla‚Äôs share value (TSLA) since mid-December ‚Äî and perhaps restore some faith in the company. Dan Ives, an analyst for Wedbush Securities and longtime Tesla bull who nonetheless recently slashed his price target for Tesla shares, said Musk is facing a ‚Äúcode red situation‚Äù if he continues his high profile role in the Trump administration. Blaming brand damage for falling sales, Ives said in a note to clients on Sunday that ‚ÄúMusk needs to leave the government, take a major step back on DOGE, and get back to being CEO of Tesla full-time.‚Äù Beyond the Musk backlash, Tesla is also facing growing competition from other EV automakers, especially in China. Bad news on any of these fronts could cause a new downward spiral for the shares. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs turned into a nightmare for Tesla and for investors,‚Äù Ives told CNN. ‚ÄúThe tariffs, the DOGE controversies, the brand damage ‚Äî it‚Äôs been a perfect storm.‚Äù In some ways, Tesla is less exposed than other automakers to Trump‚Äôs auto tariffs. It does not import cars from its two overseas factories, and it uses fewer foreign parts in its US-built vehicles than other automakers. All other major automakers also have at least some imported vehicles in their American dealerships. It has stopped taking new orders in China for its higher-priced Model S and Model X cars, which are built in California, due to China‚Äôs 125% retaliatory tariffs, but those models are only a small fraction of its overall sales. But Musk has said that the cost to Tesla will be ‚Äúnot trivial.‚Äù ‚ÄúImportant to note that Tesla is NOT unscathed here. The tariff impact on Tesla is still significant,‚Äù he said on his social media platform, X, the day after Trump‚Äôs auto tariff plans were announced. But too much criticism of tariffs during Tuesday‚Äôs call could harm his bromance with Trump. Musk became Trump‚Äôs largest financial donor last year and has been among his closest advisors since the inauguration. Tesla shares nearly doubled in value in the two months after the election as some investors and analysts hoped Musk would influence Trump to implement Tesla-friendly policies, most notably on self-driving vehicles. Conversely, strong support for Trump‚Äôs tariff policies could anger Chinese authorities and EV buyers. China is the world‚Äôs largest auto market and EV market, but its consumers are increasingly turning to rivals such as China‚Äôs own BYD. Tesla booked $20.9 billion in sales in 2024 in China, about 21% of its revenue, its second-largest market after the United States. Ives called China ‚Äúthe linchpin to the future success of Tesla.‚Äù He warned that ‚Äúthe backlash from Trump tariff policies in China and Musk‚Äôs association will be hard to understate.‚Äù Protests outside Tesla showrooms and vandalism at its facilities underscore the backlash the company and its CEO are facing. Some of the most popular investor questions on Tesla‚Äôs site touch on that issue. ‚ÄúDid Tesla experience any meaningful changes in order inflow rate in Q1 relating to all of the rumors of ‚Äòbrand damage‚Äô?‚Äù read one question endorsed by more than 1,000 investors. ‚ÄúHow is the company planning to deal with the impact of Elon‚Äôs partnership with the current administration?‚Äù asked another popular investor question. Another asked: ‚ÄúBoycotts, protests, vandalism, negative headlines, and a stock slide have been sparked by Elon Musk‚Äôs participation in changes to U.S. gov‚Äôt services & employment. Is the Tesla board discussing whether their CEO should focus fully on Tesla and leave gov‚Äôt to elected politicians?‚Äù When Musk held an all-hands meeting for Tesla employees last month, it sparked a one-week rally in its battered shares. But the rally proved short-lived. On April 2, when Tesla reported its biggest-ever year-over-year drop in quarterly sales, shares fell sharply ‚Äî until Politico ran a story later that morning that Musk would soon give up his role in the government. The news lifted shares. However, Musk and other members of the administration said the story wasn‚Äôt accurate. ‚ÄúTesla‚Äôs first quarter sales and production report causes us to think that ‚Äî if anything ‚Äî we may have underestimated the degree of consumer reaction,‚Äù JPMorgan Chase analyst Ryan Brinkman wrote in a note to clients earlier this month. Tesla did not respond to a request for comment about its sales or the impact that Musk‚Äôs role in the administration had on those sales. Musk has made ambitious promises for a fleet of self-driving robotaxis and humanoid robots, claiming that they will make the company the most valuable in the world. He had touted plans for a driverless ride-hailing service in Austin, Texas, due to start in June. But there have been no updates. Meanwhile, Uber and Google‚Äôs own driverless car unit, Waymo, has beaten Tesla to the punch with the start of its own joint driverless taxi service in that city. ‚ÄúIt would be a huge negative for the stock if he pushed back (the robotaxi plans),‚Äù Ives said. ‚ÄúWe need to hear good news there, because there‚Äôs not going to be good news in terms of earnings or what the rest of the year will look like financially.‚Äù Most stock quote data provided by BATS. Title: Hertz Stock Up 131% On Ackman‚Äôs Iffy Bet Tariffs To Boost Car Value
URL: https://www.forbes.com/sites/petercohan/2025/04/20/hertz-stock-up-131-on-ackmans-iffy-bet-tariffs-to-boost-car-value/
Time Published: 2025-04-20T17:55:35Z
Full Content:
ByPeter Cohan ByPeter Cohan, Senior Contributor. Powered by Adelaide, Forbes AI American actor Peter Falk at John F. Kennedy International Airport, New York City, 1982. (Photo by ... More Vinnie Zuffante/Getty Images) Many investors have bet against car rental company Hertz Global. But activist investor Bill Ackman has built a 19.8% stake in the company, according to an April 16 Pershing Square Capital regulatory filing, the Globe and Mail reported. Since April 15, Hertz stock ‚Äì about 40% of the company‚Äôs shares are sold short, according to the Wall Street Journal ‚Äì have surged 131%. Is now a great time to buy Hertz stock? Here are four reasons to steer clear of these shares: Hertz is pleased with Ackman‚Äôs investment. ‚ÄúI am humbled, and we should all feel encouraged by Bill Ackman‚Äôs comments and energized by the strong support shown by him,‚Äù Hertz CEO Gil West said in a letter to employees, seen by Reuters. Ackman warned investors to be wary of the stock even as he argued Hertz ‚Äì which filed for bankruptcy in 2020 and now has two business lines: renting vehicles and borrowing heavily to own vehicles -- is undervalued, noted the Globe And Mail. I requested a comment from Hertz and will update this post if I receive a response. On social media, Ackman ‚Äì who has a large following among individual investors and owns a big stake in Uber ‚Äì gave three reasons why shares of Hertz could be worth $30 per share by 2029, the Globe And Mail reported. These include the following: While Ackman is right about the industry structure being concentrated, his numbers appear high. Rather than 95% of the market, their combined market share is 83% ‚Äì Enterprise (39%), Hertz (22%), and Avis/Budget (22%), according to Market.us. Also, I question how successful Hertz‚Äôs turnaround is and whether tariffs and the Uber/Hertz partnership will boost Hertz‚Äôs value. After hosting a revolving door of CEOs, Hertz has recently contracted unprofitably. Hertz entered bankruptcy in May 2020 and emerged on July 1, 2021, under Paul Stone‚Äôs leadership ‚Äì "returning about $8 a share to stockholders in its exit from chapter 11," noted the Journal. Mark Fields, who served as interim CEO from October 5, 2021, made the decision for Hertz to order 100,000 Teslas. Despite TV ads from Tom Brady promoting the Tesla fleet, consumers did not take the bait, the Journal reported. Hertz‚Äôs operating costs rose and in February 2022 Fields was replaced by Goldman Sachs veteran Stephen Scherr who built up the EV fleet. In December 2022, Hertz took a "$168 million charge to settle 364 claims related to the company falsely reporting rental cars as stolen," according to CNN. On April 1, 2024, West ‚Äì who was previously Delta Airlines chief operating officer -- replaced Scherr as CEO. West cut operating costs by selling a large portion of Hertz‚Äôs EV fleet and the associated remote charging network ‚Äì taking a $200 million writedown, the Journal wrote. The EV blunder reduced Hertz‚Äôs earnings, and the company has reported net losses for the last couple of years ‚Äì including quarterly losses for the most recent five quarters ending December 2024. Moreover, during that time the rental car company‚Äôs debt load has increased 6% to $18.3 billion, its equity has plunged 95% to $153 million, and its revenue fell 6% to about $2 billion in the final quarter of 2024, noted the Journal. Ackman sees tariffs boosting the value of Hertz‚Äôs fleet of used late-model vehicles. He als0 expects a partnership with Uber to increase the companies‚Äô fleet utilization and profitability. He forecasts President Donald Trump‚Äôs 25% levy on imported automobiles will boost the value of Hertz‚Äôs 500,000 vehicles by 10% ‚Äì increasing their value to $13.2 billion. Moreover, the ‚Äú$1.2 billion gain on its auto assets is equivalent to approximately half of the company‚Äôs current market capitalization,‚Äù Ackman said in an X post featured by Bloomberg. Ackman‚Äôs 10% is at the high end of a range of used car price increase estimates. For example, one analyst expects used car prices to rise between a 5% and 10% in the near future, according to iSeeCars. Of course if that happens, I doubt Ackman expects Hertz to sell all 500,000 of those vehicles. So, as the company replaces older vehicles with new ones subject to tariffs, it is unclear how much of that $1.2 billion value increase the company will capture. Ackman is counting on West to boost Hertz‚Äôs stock price. Getting there would require the company to reach the CEO‚Äôs operating targets which include $1,500 in revenue per unit, around $30-per-vehicle operating expense, about $300 in depreciation per unit, and an increase in fleet utilization from 80% to 85%, Ackman noted on X. Ackman also floated the idea of Hertz managing a fleet of self-driving vehicles ‚Äì in its 11,200 global locations ‚Äì for Uber which is ‚Äúexcited to brainstorm on how we can expand on our relationship,‚Äù CEO Dara Khosrowshahi posted on X. I question the realism of West‚Äôs operating targets. Many economists see tariffs causing an economic slowdown. If that happens, consumers and businesses could cut back on travel ‚Äì thus lowering Hertz‚Äôs revenue per unit. Moreover, with tariffs raising the price of auto parts, vehicle operating expenses could go up rather than fall. Hertz faces other threats ‚Äì notably a $272 million legal judgment from bondholders who claim the company should have paid them interest during its time in chapter 11. Hertz is appealing the judgment to the Supreme Court. ‚ÄúWhile the decline of the COVID-19 pandemic and the resumption of global travel rendered Hertz solvent despite the high stakes of this dispute, the nearly $272 million at issue here could make the difference between solvency and insolvency in many cases,‚Äù Hertz lawyers wrote in an April 4 petition to the high court featured in a Bloomberg report. Bondholders remain wary of Hertz‚Äôs junior bonds. They still trade ‚Äúaround 50 or 60 cents on the dollar,‚Äù the Journal reported. Hertz shares are way overvalued in the view of Wall Street. Four analysts who cover the stock set a price target of $2.93 ‚Äì suggesting the rental car company stock is 181% over-valued. Short sellers may have contributed to this overvaluation by scrambling to repay the shares they borrowed ‚Äì assuming some of them purchased the stock in a rising market following Ackman‚Äôs announcement of his 19.8% stake. I agree with Ackman‚Äôs concluding thoughts. ‚ÄúInvesting is risky,‚Äù he wrote on X. ‚ÄúThere are no guarantees of a successful outcome. Caveat emptor.‚Äù Consider avoiding Hertz stock.

UPDATED 06:33 EDT / APRIL 20 2025 BREAKING ANALYSIS by Dave Vellante Breaking Analysis is a weekly editorial program combining knowledge from SiliconANGLE‚Äôs theCUBE with spending data from Enterprise Technology Research. Branded as theCUBE Insights, Powered by ETR, the program is our opportunity to share independent, unfiltered editorial with SiliconANGLE, theCUBE and Wikibon communities. The program and conclusions we produce are data-driven, tapping ETR‚Äôs proprietary spending data set. Episode 221 ‚Äì Nvidia, Broadcom and the expanding breadth of AI ‚Äì We attended both Nvidia Corp.‚Äôs GTC conference and Broadcom Inc.‚Äôs investor day this week where the artificial intelligence platform shift was on full display. In our view, GTC24 was the most important event in the history of the technology industry, surpassing Steve Jobs‚Äô iPod and iPhone launches. The event was not the largest but, in our opinion, it was the most significant in terms of its reach, vision, ecosystem impact and broad-based recognition that the AI era will permanently change the world. Meanwhile, Broadcom‚Äôs first investor day underscored both the importance of the AI era and the highly differentiated strategies and paths that Nvidia and Broadcom are each taking. We believe Nvidia and Broadcom are currently the two best-positioned companies to capitalize on the AI wave and will each dominate their respective markets for the better part of a decade. But importantly, we see them each as enablers of a broader ecosystem that collectively will create more value than either of these firms will in and of themselves. In this Breaking Analysis, we will share our perspectives on the state of AI and how Nvidia and Broadcom are each leading the way with dramatically different but overlapping strategies that may be headed for an eventual collision course. Watch the full video analysis. Episode 209 ‚Äì Moving beyond separation of compute & storage‚Ä¶Journey to the 6th data platform ‚Äì We believe today‚Äôs so-called modern data stack, as currently envisioned, will be challenged by emerging use cases and AI-infused apps that begin to represent the real world, in real time, at massive data scale. Episode 196 ‚Äì Copilot or competitor ‚Äì How gen AI bolsters and buffets UiPath‚Äôs Northstar ‚Äì UiPath‚Äôs recent earnings beat and raise provides some evidence that thus far, Gen AI has not been dilutive for the company. Episode 186 ‚Äì Connecting the Dots on Snowflake‚Äôs Data Cloud Ambitions ‚Äì Over the past several months we‚Äôve produced a number of in-depth analyses laying out our mental model for the future of data platforms. There are two core themes: 1) Data from people, places, things, and activities in the real world drives applications, not people typing into a UI; and 2) Informing and automating decisions means all data must be accessible. That drives a change from data locked in application silos to application logic being embedded in a platform that manages an end-to-end representation of an enterprise in its data. This week‚Äôs Snowflake Summit further confirmed our expectations with a strong top line message of ‚ÄúAll Data / All Workloads‚Äù and a technical foundation that supports an expanded number of ways to access data. Squinting through the messaging and firehose of product announcements, we believe Snowflake‚Äôs core differentiation is its emerging ability to be a complete platform for data applications. Just about all competitors either analyze data or manage data. But no one vendor truly does both. To be precise, managing data doesn‚Äôt mean running pipelines or serving analytic queries or AI/ML models. It means managing operational data so that analytics can inform or automate operational activities captured in transactions. With data as the application foundation, the platform needs robust governance. In this week‚Äôs Breaking Analysis, we try to connect the dots between Snowflake‚Äôs high level messaging and its technical foundation to better understand the core value it brings to customers and partners. Episode 142 ‚Äì What we hope to learn at Supercloud22 ‚Äì The term supercloud is relatively new, but the concepts behind it have been bubbling for years. Early last decade when NIST put forth its original definition of cloud computing, it said services had to be accessible over a public network‚Ä¶essentially cutting the on-prem crowd out of the conversation. A guy named Chuck Hollis, a CTO at EMC and prolific blogger objected to that criterion and laid out his vision for what he termed a private cloud. In that post he showed a workload running both on premises and in a public cloud, sharing the underlying resources in an automated and seamless manner ‚Äì what later became more broadly known as hybrid cloud. That vision, as we now know, really never materialized and we were left with multi-cloud‚Ä¶sets of largely incompatible and disconnected cloud services running in separate silos. The point is, what Hollis put forth ‚Äì i.e. the ability to abstract underlying infrastructure complexity and run workloads across multiple heterogeneous estates with an identical experience ‚Äì is what supercloud is all about. Watch the full video analysis. Episode 141 ‚Äì How the cloud is changing security defenses in the 2020s ‚Äì AThe rapid pace of cloud adoption has changed the way organizations approach cybersecurity. The DevSecOps team has emerged as the critical link between strategy and execution, while audit becomes the ‚Äúfree safety‚Äù in the equation ‚Äì i.e. the last line of defense. We used the term supercloud to describe an abstraction layer that resides above and across hyperscale infrastructure, connects on premises workloads and eventually stretches to the edge. Our premise is that supercloud goes beyond running services in native mode on each individual cloud. Episode 129- What you May not Know About the Dell Snowflake Deal ‚Äì In the pre-cloud era, hardware companies would run benchmarks showing how database and application performance ran best on their systems relative to competitors and previous generation boxes. Episode 127- Does Hardware Still Matter ‚Äì The ascendancy of cloud and SaaS has shone new light on how organizations think about, pay for, and value hardware. Title: Bill Ackman Is Betting on Car Rental Giant Hertz to be a Tariff Winner
URL: https://observer.com/2025/04/bill-ackman-hertz-tariff/
Time Published: 2025-04-18T21:55:50Z
Full Content:
President Trump‚Äôs auto tariffs may have sparked fears of price hikes among consumers, but some car-buying businesses could actually benefit, according to investor Bill Ackman. The Pershing Square Capital Management founder made a bullish case for Hertz yesterday (April 17), after revealing a nearly 20 percent stake in the car rental company. Thank you for signing up! By clicking submit, you agree to our <a href="http://observermedia.com/terms">terms of service</a> and acknowledge we may use your information to send you emails, product samples, and promotions on this website and other properties. You can opt out anytime. ‚ÄúHertz is uniquely well-positioned in the current tariff environment,‚Äù Ackman wrote in a post on X. As 25 percent levies on imported cars drive up used car prices, the value of Hertz‚Äôs fleet‚Äîaround 500,000 vehicles worth roughly $1.2 billion‚Äîwill rise in tandem, he argued. A 10 percent increase in used car values would boost the company‚Äôs fleet value by $1.2 billion, nearly half of Hertz‚Äôs current market cap. Shares of Hertz surged more than 44 percent following the disclosure of Ackman‚Äôs 19.8 percent stake. Hertz has had its fair share of troubles in the past few years. After declaring bankruptcy in 2020 during the Covid-19 pandemic, the company pivoted hard to electric vehicles‚Äîplacing a costly bet on Tesla that fell flat due to soft demand. In February, Hertz reported a $2.9 billion loss for 2024 and said it had sold off 30,000 EVs from its fleet. Ackman, who began building his position in the company last year, is betting on a successful turnaround‚Äîand a stock price that could more than triple to $30 per share by 2029. That task falls in the hands of Hertz‚Äôs new CEO Gil West, who stepped into the role in early 2024. West is focused on ‚Äúrotating Hertz‚Äôs fleet, increasing unit revenues, and reducing operating costs‚Äù to ‚Äúsignificantly improve profit margins over the next several years,‚Äù Ackman said. In a note to employees today (April 18), West praised Ackman‚Äôs support and described it as an ‚Äútestament to our progress,‚Äù according to Bloomberg. Ackman also highlighted Hertz‚Äôs advantageous position in an ‚Äúoligopoly.‚Äù He noted that Hertz and its two rivals, Enterprise and Avis, currently control around 95 percent of the car rental market. Praising Enterprise as the best-run of the trio, Ackman pointed to its estimated 20 percent profit margin as evidence ‚Äúthat the car rental business can be very profitable.‚Äù Ackman floated the possibility of a partnership between Hertz and Uber (UBER)‚Äîanother company Pershing Square has taken a sizable stake in. ‚ÄúWhat if [Uber] partnered with Hertz on an [autonomous vehicle] fleet roll out over time?‚Äù he suggested, hinting at a potential conversation with Uber CEO Dara Khosrowshahi. With its large fleet, global footprint and expertise in vehicle maintenance, Hertz would ‚Äúmake it an ideal partner‚Äù for Uber, Ackman said. Uber seems open to the idea. ‚ÄúHertz has been a great partner of ours,‚Äù Khosrowshahi responded on X. ‚ÄúExcited to brainstorm on how we can expand our relationship!‚Äù We get it: you like to have control of your own internet experience.
